<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Selenium</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">

<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the chemical element. For the software testing framework, see <a href="/wiki/Selenium_(software)" title="Selenium (software)">Selenium (software)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">chemical element with atomic number 34</div><table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption><span style="white-space:nowrap">Selenium,  <sub><span style="font-size:smaller;">34</span></sub>Se</span></caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">

<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span></span>/</a></span></span> <wbr/>​<span class="nowrap">(<a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">sə-<span style="font-size:90%">LEE</span>-nee-əm</i></a>)</span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Appearance</th><td>

<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_selenium" title="Template:Infobox selenium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_selenium" title="Template talk:Infobox selenium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_selenium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div> | <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Selenium</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Se</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 34. It is a <a href="/wiki/Nonmetal" title="Nonmetal">nonmetal</a> (more rarely considered a <a href="/wiki/Metalloid" title="Metalloid">metalloid</a>) with properties that are intermediate between the elements above and below in the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tellurium" title="Tellurium">tellurium</a>, and also has similarities to <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>. It rarely occurs in its elemental state or as pure ore compounds in the Earth's crust. Selenium (from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> <span lang="el" title="Greek language text">σελήνη</span> (selḗnē) "Moon") was discovered in 1817 by <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius" title="Jöns Jacob Berzelius">Jöns Jacob Berzelius</a>, who noted the similarity of the new element to the previously discovered <a href="/wiki/Tellurium" title="Tellurium">tellurium</a> (named for the Earth).
</p><p>Selenium is found in <a href="/wiki/Category:Sulfide_minerals" title="Category:Sulfide minerals">metal sulfide ores</a>, where it partially replaces the sulfur. Commercially, selenium is produced as a byproduct in the refining of these ores, most often during production. Minerals that are pure selenide or selenate compounds are known but rare. The chief commercial uses for selenium today are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glassmaking" title="Glassmaking">glassmaking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigments</a>. Selenium is a <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductor</a> and is used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photocell" title="Photocell">photocells</a>. Applications in <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">electronics</a>, once important, have been mostly replaced with <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> semiconductor devices. Selenium is still used in a few types of DC power <a href="/wiki/Surge_protector" title="Surge protector">surge protectors</a> and one type of <a href="/wiki/Fluorescence" title="Fluorescence">fluorescent</a> <a href="/wiki/Quantum_dot" title="Quantum dot">quantum dot</a>.
</p><p>Selenium salts are toxic in large amounts, but trace amounts are necessary for cellular function in many organisms, including all animals. Selenium is an ingredient in many multivitamins and other dietary supplements, including <a href="/wiki/Infant_formula" title="Infant formula">infant formula</a>.  It is a component of the antioxidant enzymes <a href="/wiki/Glutathione_peroxidase" title="Glutathione peroxidase">glutathione peroxidase</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thioredoxin_reductase" title="Thioredoxin reductase">thioredoxin reductase</a> (which indirectly reduce certain <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">oxidized</a> molecules in animals and some plants). It is also found in three <a href="/wiki/Deiodinase" title="Deiodinase">deiodinase</a> enzymes, which convert one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thyroid_hormone" title="Thyroid hormone">thyroid hormone</a> to another. Selenium requirements in plants differ by species, with some plants requiring relatively large amounts and others apparently requiring none.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruyle_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruyle-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Selenium_trigonal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="547" data-file-width="704" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Selenium_trigonal.jpg/170px-Selenium_trigonal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Selenium_trigonal.jpg/255px-Selenium_trigonal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Selenium_trigonal.jpg/340px-Selenium_trigonal.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Selenium_trigonal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of hexagonal (gray) selenium</div></div></div>
<p>Selenium forms several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allotrope" title="Allotrope">allotropes</a> that interconvert with temperature changes, depending somewhat on the rate of temperature change. When prepared in chemical reactions, selenium is usually an <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_solid" title="Amorphous solid">amorphous</a>, brick-red powder. When rapidly melted, it forms the black, vitreous form, usually sold commercially as beads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-house2008_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-house2008-6">[6]</a></sup> The structure of black selenium is irregular and complex and consists of polymeric rings with up to 1000 atoms per ring. Black Se is a brittle, lustrous solid that is slightly soluble in <a href="/wiki/Carbon_disulfide" title="Carbon disulfide">CS<sub>2</sub></a>. Upon heating, it softens at 50 °C and converts to gray selenium at 180 °C; the transformation temperature is reduced by presence of halogens and <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ge_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-ge-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The red α, β, and γ forms are produced from solutions of black selenium by varying the evaporation rate of the solvent (usually CS<sub>2</sub>). They all have relatively low, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monoclinic" title="Monoclinic">monoclinic</a> crystal symmetries and contain nearly identical puckered Se<sub>8</sub> rings with different arrangements, as in <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>. The packing is most dense in the α form. In the Se<sub>8</sub> rings, the Se-Se distance is 233.5 pm and Se-Se-Se angle is 105.7°. Other selenium allotropes may contain Se<sub>6</sub> or Se<sub>7</sub> rings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ge_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-ge-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most stable and dense form of selenium is gray and has a hexagonal crystal lattice consisting of helical polymeric chains, where the Se-Se distance is 237.3 pm and Se-Se-Se angle is 130.1°. The minimum distance between chains is 343.6 pm. Gray Se is formed by mild heating of other allotropes, by slow cooling of molten Se, or by condensing Se vapor just below the melting point. Whereas other Se forms are insulators, gray Se is a semiconductor showing appreciable <a href="/wiki/Photoconductivity" title="Photoconductivity">photoconductivity</a>. Unlike the other allotropes, it is insoluble in CS<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ge_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-ge-7">[7]</a></sup> It resists oxidation by air and is not attacked by nonoxidizing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acids" title="Acids">acids</a>. With strong reducing agents, it forms polyselenides. Selenium does not exhibit the changes in viscosity that sulfur undergoes when gradually heated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-house2008_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-house2008-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Optical_properties">Optical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Optical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Owing to its use as a photoconductor in flat-panel x-ray detectors (see <a href="#Photoconductors">below</a>), the optical properties of amorphous selenium (α-Se) thin films have been the subject of intense research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jafar2016_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jafar2016-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saleh2017_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saleh2017-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minkov2017_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Minkov2017-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_selenium" title="Isotopes of selenium">Isotopes of selenium</a></div>
<p>Selenium has seven natural <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Selenium-79" title="Selenium-79"><sup>79</sup>Se</a>, which occurs in minute quantities in <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> ores, as well as 23 other synthetic isotopes.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_compounds">Chemical compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Chemical compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Selenium_compounds" title="Category:Selenium compounds">Category:Selenium compounds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Organoselenium_chemistry" title="Organoselenium chemistry">organoselenium chemistry</a></div>
<p>Selenium compounds commonly exist in the <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a> −2, +2, +4, and +6.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chalcogen_compounds">Chalcogen compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Chalcogen compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Selenium forms two <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxides</a>: <a href="/wiki/Selenium_dioxide" title="Selenium dioxide">selenium dioxide</a> (SeO<sub>2</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Selenium_trioxide" title="Selenium trioxide">selenium trioxide</a> (SeO<sub>3</sub>). Selenium dioxide is formed by the reaction of elemental selenium with oxygen:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-house2008_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-house2008-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>Se<sub>8</sub> + 8 O<sub>2</sub> → 8 SeO<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Selenium-dioxide-chain-3D-balls.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="333" data-file-width="1000" height="73" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Selenium-dioxide-chain-3D-balls.png/220px-Selenium-dioxide-chain-3D-balls.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Selenium-dioxide-chain-3D-balls.png/330px-Selenium-dioxide-chain-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Selenium-dioxide-chain-3D-balls.png/440px-Selenium-dioxide-chain-3D-balls.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Selenium-dioxide-chain-3D-balls.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of the polymer SeO<sub>2</sub>: The (pyramidal) Se atoms are yellow.</div></div></div>
<p>It is a <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymeric</a> solid that forms monomeric SeO<sub>2</sub> molecules in the gas phase. It dissolves in water to form <a href="/wiki/Selenous_acid" title="Selenous acid">selenous acid</a>, H<sub>2</sub>SeO<sub>3</sub>. Selenous acid can also be made directly by oxidizing elemental selenium with <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wiberg_holleman_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-wiberg_holleman-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>3 Se + 4 HNO<sub>3</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O → 3 H<sub>2</sub>SeO<sub>3</sub> + 4 NO</dd></dl>
<p>Unlike sulfur, which forms a stable <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_trioxide" title="Sulfur trioxide">trioxide</a>, selenium trioxide is thermodynamically unstable and decomposes to the dioxide above 185 °C:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-house2008_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-house2008-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wiberg_holleman_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-wiberg_holleman-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 SeO<sub>3</sub> → 2 SeO<sub>2</sub> + O<sub>2</sub> (ΔH = −54 kJ/mol)</dd></dl>
<p>Selenium trioxide is produced in the laboratory by the reaction of <a href="/wiki/Anhydrous" title="Anhydrous">anhydrous</a> <a href="/wiki/Potassium_selenate" title="Potassium selenate">potassium selenate</a> (K<sub>2</sub>SeO<sub>4</sub>) and sulfur trioxide (SO<sub>3</sub>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">Salts</a> of selenous acid are called selenites. These include <a href="/wiki/Silver_selenite" title="Silver selenite">silver selenite</a> (Ag<sub>2</sub>SeO<sub>3</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_selenite" title="Sodium selenite">sodium selenite</a> (Na<sub>2</sub>SeO<sub>3</sub>).
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">Hydrogen sulfide</a> reacts with aqueous selenous acid to produce <a href="/wiki/Selenium_disulfide" title="Selenium disulfide">selenium disulfide</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>H<sub>2</sub>SeO<sub>3</sub> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>S → SeS<sub>2</sub> + 3 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>Selenium disulfide consists of 8-membered rings. It has an approximate composition of SeS<sub>2</sub>, with individual rings varying in composition, such as Se<sub>4</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and Se<sub>2</sub>S<sub>6</sub>. Selenium disulfide has been used in shampoo as an anti<a href="/wiki/Dandruff" title="Dandruff">dandruff</a> agent, an inhibitor in polymer chemistry, a glass dye, and a reducing agent in <a href="/wiki/Fireworks" title="Fireworks">fireworks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wiberg_holleman_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-wiberg_holleman-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Selenium trioxide may be synthesized by dehydrating <a href="/wiki/Selenic_acid" title="Selenic acid">selenic acid</a>, H<sub>2</sub>SeO<sub>4</sub>, which is itself produced by the oxidation of selenium dioxide with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">hydrogen peroxide</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>SeO<sub>2</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> → H<sub>2</sub>SeO<sub>4</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Hot, concentrated selenic acid can react with gold to form gold(III) selenate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Halogen_compounds">Halogen compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Halogen compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Iodide" title="Iodide">Iodides</a> of selenium are not well known. The only stable <a href="/wiki/Chloride" title="Chloride">chloride</a> is <a href="/wiki/Selenium_monochloride" title="Selenium monochloride">selenium monochloride</a> (Se<sub>2</sub>Cl<sub>2</sub>), which might be better known as selenium(I) chloride; the corresponding <a href="/wiki/Bromide" title="Bromide">bromide</a> is also known. These species are structurally analogous to the corresponding <a href="/wiki/Disulfur_dichloride" title="Disulfur dichloride">disulfur dichloride</a>. Selenium dichloride is an important reagent in the preparation of selenium compounds (e.g. the preparation of Se<sub>7</sub>). It is prepared by treating selenium with <a href="/wiki/Sulfuryl_chloride" title="Sulfuryl chloride">sulfuryl chloride</a> (SO<sub>2</sub>Cl<sub>2</sub>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Selenium reacts with <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Selenium_hexafluoride" title="Selenium hexafluoride">selenium hexafluoride</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>Se<sub>8</sub> + 24 F<sub>2</sub> → 8 SeF<sub>6</sub></dd></dl>
<p>In comparison with its sulfur counterpart (<a href="/wiki/Sulfur_hexafluoride" title="Sulfur hexafluoride">sulfur hexafluoride</a>), <a href="/wiki/Selenium_hexafluoride" title="Selenium hexafluoride">selenium hexafluoride</a> (SeF<sub>6</sub>) is more reactive and is a toxic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulmonary" title="Pulmonary">pulmonary</a> irritant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
Some of the selenium oxyhalides, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selenium_oxyfluoride" title="Selenium oxyfluoride">selenium oxyfluoride</a> (SeOF<sub>2</sub>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selenium_oxychloride" title="Selenium oxychloride">selenium oxychloride</a> (SeOCl<sub>2</sub>) have been used as specialty solvents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-house2008_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-house2008-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Selenides">Selenides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Selenides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Analogous to the behavior of other chalcogens, selenium forms <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_selenide" title="Hydrogen selenide">hydrogen selenide</a>, H<sub>2</sub>Se. It is a strongly <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/odiferous" title="wikt:odiferous">odiferous</a>, toxic, and colorless gas. It is more acidic than H<sub>2</sub>S. In solution it ionizes to HSe<sup>−</sup>. The selenide dianion Se<sup>2−</sup> forms a variety of compounds, including the minerals from which selenium is obtained commercially. Illustrative selenides include <a href="/wiki/Mercury_selenide" title="Mercury selenide">mercury selenide</a> (HgSe), <a href="/wiki/Lead_selenide" title="Lead selenide">lead selenide</a> (PbSe), <a href="/wiki/Zinc_selenide" title="Zinc selenide">zinc selenide</a> (ZnSe), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper_indium_gallium_diselenide" title="Copper indium gallium diselenide">copper indium gallium diselenide</a> (Cu(Ga,In)Se<sub>2</sub>). These materials are <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductors</a>. With highly electropositive metals, such as <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>, these selenides are prone to hydrolysis:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-house2008_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-house2008-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>Al<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> + 3 H<sub>2</sub>O → Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> + 3 H<sub>2</sub>Se</dd></dl>
<p>Alkali metal selenides react with selenium to form polyselenides, <span class="chemf nowrap">Se<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">n</sub></span></span>, which exist as chains.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_compounds">Other compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Other compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Tetraselenium tetranitride, Se<sub>4</sub>N<sub>4</sub>, is an explosive orange compound analogous to <a href="/wiki/Tetrasulfur_tetranitride" title="Tetrasulfur tetranitride">tetrasulfur tetranitride</a> (S<sub>4</sub>N<sub>4</sub>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-house2008_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-house2008-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> It can be synthesized by the reaction of <a href="/wiki/Selenium_tetrachloride" title="Selenium tetrachloride">selenium tetrachloride</a> (SeCl<sub>4</sub>) with <a href="/wiki/Metal_bis(trimethylsilyl)amides" title="Metal bis(trimethylsilyl)amides"><span class="chemf nowrap">[((CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>Si)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>N]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>Se</span></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Selenium reacts with <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">cyanides</a> to yield <a href="/wiki/Potassium_selenocyanate" title="Potassium selenocyanate">selenocyanates</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-house2008_6-7"><a href="#cite_note-house2008-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>8 KCN + Se<sub>8</sub> → 8 KSeCN</dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organoselenium_compounds">Organoselenium compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Organoselenium compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organoselenium_chemistry" title="Organoselenium chemistry">Organoselenium chemistry</a></div>
<p>Selenium, especially in the II oxidation state, forms stable bonds to <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, which are structurally analogous to the corresponding <a href="/wiki/Organosulfur_compounds" title="Organosulfur compounds">organosulfur compounds</a>. Especially common are selenides (R<sub>2</sub>Se, analogues of <a href="/wiki/Thioether" title="Thioether">thioethers</a>), diselenides (R<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>2</sub>, analogues of <a href="/wiki/Disulfide" title="Disulfide">disulfides</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Selenol" title="Selenol">selenols</a> (RSeH, analogues of <a href="/wiki/Thiol" title="Thiol">thiols</a>). Representatives of selenides, diselenides, and selenols include respectively <a href="/wiki/Selenomethionine" title="Selenomethionine">selenomethionine</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diphenyldiselenide" title="Diphenyldiselenide">diphenyldiselenide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Benzeneselenol" title="Benzeneselenol">benzeneselenol</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Sulfoxide" title="Sulfoxide">sulfoxide</a> in sulfur chemistry is represented in selenium chemistry by the selenoxides (formula RSe(O)R), which are intermediates in organic synthesis, as illustrated by the <a href="/wiki/Selenoxide_elimination" title="Selenoxide elimination">selenoxide elimination</a> reaction. Consistent with trends indicated by the <a href="/wiki/Double_bond_rule" title="Double bond rule">double bond rule</a>, selenoketones, R(C=Se)R, and selenaldehydes, R(C=Se)H, are rarely observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Selenium (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> σελήνη <i><a href="/wiki/Selene" title="Selene">selene</a></i> meaning "Moon") was discovered in 1817 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jakob_Berzelius" title="Jöns Jakob Berzelius">Jöns Jakob Berzelius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johan_Gottlieb_Gahn" title="Johan Gottlieb Gahn">Johan Gottlieb Gahn</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Both chemists owned a chemistry plant near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gripsholm" title="Gripsholm">Gripsholm</a>, Sweden, producing <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Lead_chamber_process" title="Lead chamber process">lead chamber process</a>. The pyrite from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Copper_Mountain" title="Great Copper Mountain">Falun mine</a> created a red precipitate in the lead chambers which was presumed to be an arsenic compound, so the pyrite's use to make acid was discontinued. Berzelius and Gahn wanted to use the pyrite and they also observed that the red precipitate gave off a smell like <a href="/wiki/Horseradish" title="Horseradish">horseradish</a> when burned. This smell was not typical of arsenic, but a similar odor was known from <a href="/wiki/Tellurium" title="Tellurium">tellurium</a> compounds. Hence, Berzelius's first letter to <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Marcet" title="Alexander Marcet">Alexander Marcet</a> stated that this was a tellurium compound. However, the lack of tellurium compounds in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Copper_Mountain" title="Great Copper Mountain">Falun mine</a> minerals eventually led Berzelius to reanalyze the red precipitate, and in 1818 he wrote a second letter to Marcet describing a newly found element similar to <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> and tellurium. Because of its similarity to tellurium, named for the Earth, Berzelius named the new element after the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1873, <a href="/wiki/Willoughby_Smith" title="Willoughby Smith">Willoughby Smith</a> found that the electrical resistance of grey selenium was dependent on the ambient light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> This led to its use as a cell for sensing light. The first commercial products using selenium were developed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Werner_Siemens" title="Werner Siemens">Werner Siemens</a> in the mid-1870s. The selenium cell was used in the <a href="/wiki/Photophone" title="Photophone">photophone</a> developed by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Graham_Bell" title="Alexander Graham Bell">Alexander Graham Bell</a> in 1879. Selenium transmits an electric current proportional to the amount of light falling on its surface. This phenomenon was used in the design of <a href="/wiki/Light_meter" title="Light meter">light meters</a> and similar devices. Selenium's semiconductor properties found numerous other applications in electronics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The development of <a href="/wiki/Selenium_rectifier" title="Selenium rectifier">selenium rectifiers</a> began during the early 1930s, and these replaced <a href="/wiki/Copper_oxide" title="Copper oxide">copper oxide</a> rectifiers because they were more efficient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> These lasted in commercial applications until the 1970s, following which they were replaced with less expensive and even more efficient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silicon_rectifier" title="Silicon rectifier">silicon rectifiers</a>.
</p><p>Selenium came to medical notice later because of its toxicity to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_being" title="Human being">human beings</a> working in industries. Selenium was also recognized as an important veterinary toxin, which is seen in animals that have eaten high-selenium plants. In 1954, the first hints of specific biological functions of selenium were discovered in <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> by biochemist, <a href="/wiki/Jane_Gibson" title="Jane Gibson">Jane Pinsent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> It was discovered to be essential for mammalian life in 1957.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> In the 1970s, it was shown to be present in two independent sets of <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a>. This was followed by the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Selenocysteine" title="Selenocysteine">selenocysteine</a> in proteins. During the 1980s,  selenocysteine was shown to be encoded by the <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">codon UGA</a>. The recoding mechanism was worked out first in <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> and then in <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> (see <a href="/wiki/SECIS_element" title="SECIS element">SECIS element</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Selenium_in_sandstone_Westwater_Canyon_Section_23_Mine_Grants,_New_Mexico.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1658" data-file-width="2236" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Selenium_in_sandstone_Westwater_Canyon_Section_23_Mine_Grants%2C_New_Mexico.jpg/220px-Selenium_in_sandstone_Westwater_Canyon_Section_23_Mine_Grants%2C_New_Mexico.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Selenium_in_sandstone_Westwater_Canyon_Section_23_Mine_Grants%2C_New_Mexico.jpg/330px-Selenium_in_sandstone_Westwater_Canyon_Section_23_Mine_Grants%2C_New_Mexico.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Selenium_in_sandstone_Westwater_Canyon_Section_23_Mine_Grants%2C_New_Mexico.jpg/440px-Selenium_in_sandstone_Westwater_Canyon_Section_23_Mine_Grants%2C_New_Mexico.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Selenium_in_sandstone_Westwater_Canyon_Section_23_Mine_Grants,_New_Mexico.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Native selenium in sandstone, from a uranium mine near <a href="/wiki/Grants,_New_Mexico" title="Grants, New Mexico">Grants, New Mexico</a></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Selenide_minerals" title="Category:Selenide minerals">Category:Selenide minerals</a>.</div>
<p>Native (i.e., elemental) selenium is a rare mineral, which does not usually form good crystals, but, when it does, they are steep rhombohedra or tiny acicular (hair-like) crystals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Isolation of selenium is often complicated by the presence of other compounds and elements.
</p><p>Selenium occurs naturally in a number of inorganic forms, including <a href="/wiki/Selenide" title="Selenide">selenide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Selenate" title="Selenate">selenate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Selenite_(ion)" title="Selenite (ion)">selenite</a>, but these minerals are rare. The common mineral <a href="/wiki/Selenite_(mineral)" title="Selenite (mineral)">selenite</a> is not a selenium mineral, and contains no <a href="/wiki/Selenite_(ion)" title="Selenite (ion)">selenite ion</a>, but is rather a type of <a href="/wiki/Gypsum" title="Gypsum">gypsum</a> (calcium sulfate hydrate) named like selenium for the moon well before the discovery of selenium. Selenium is most commonly found as an impurity, replacing a small part of the sulfur in sulfide ores of many metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geosel1_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-geosel1-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geosel2_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-geosel2-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>In living systems, selenium is found in the amino acids <a href="/wiki/Selenomethionine" title="Selenomethionine">selenomethionine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Selenocysteine" title="Selenocysteine">selenocysteine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Methylselenocysteine" title="Methylselenocysteine">methylselenocysteine</a>. In these compounds, selenium plays a role analogous to that of sulfur. Another naturally occurring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organoselenium_compound" title="Organoselenium compound">organoselenium compound</a> is dimethyl selenide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biol1_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-biol1-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biol1a_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-biol1a-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Certain solids are selenium-rich, and selenium can be <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">bioconcentrated</a> by some plants. In soils, selenium most often occurs in soluble forms such as selenate (analogous to sulfate), which are leached into rivers very easily by runoff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geosel1_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-geosel1-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geosel2_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-geosel2-43">[43]</a></sup> Ocean water contains significant amounts of selenium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Anthropogenic sources of selenium include coal burning, and the mining and smelting of sulfide ores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Selenium is most commonly produced from <a href="/wiki/Selenide" title="Selenide">selenide</a> in many <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfide</a> <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ores</a>, such as those of <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>. Electrolytic metal refining is particularly productive of selenium as a byproduct, obtained from the <a href="/wiki/Anode" title="Anode">anode</a> mud of copper refineries. Another source was the mud from the <a href="/wiki/Lead_chamber_process" title="Lead chamber process">lead chambers</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> plants, a process that is no longer used. Selenium can be refined from these muds by a number of methods. However, most elemental selenium comes as a byproduct of <a href="/wiki/Refining_(metallurgy)" title="Refining (metallurgy)">refining</a> copper or producing <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Since its invention, <a href="/wiki/Solvent_extraction_and_electrowinning" title="Solvent extraction and electrowinning">solvent extraction and electrowinning</a> (SX/EW) production of copper produces an increasing share of the worldwide copper supply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> This changes the availability of selenium because only a comparably small part of the selenium in the ore is leached with the copper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naumov_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Naumov-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Industrial production of selenium usually involves the extraction of <a href="/wiki/Selenium_dioxide" title="Selenium dioxide">selenium dioxide</a> from residues obtained during the purification of copper. Common production from the residue then begins by oxidation with <a href="/wiki/Sodium_carbonate" title="Sodium carbonate">sodium carbonate</a> to produce selenium dioxide, which is mixed with water and <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acidified</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Selenous_acid" title="Selenous acid">selenous acid</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a> step). Selenous acid is bubbled with <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> (<a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reduction</a> step) to give elemental selenium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>About 2,000 tonnes of selenium were produced in 2011 worldwide, mostly in Germany (650 t), Japan (630 t), Belgium (200 t), and Russia (140 t), and the total reserves were estimated at 93,000 tonnes. These data exclude two major producers, the United States and China.  A previous sharp increase was observed in 2004 from 4–5 to $27/lb. The price was relatively stable during 2004–2010 at about US$30 per pound (in 100-pound lots) but increased to $65 /lb in 2011. The consumption in 2010 was divided as follows: metallurgy – 30%, glass manufacturing – 30%, agriculture – 10%, chemicals and pigments – 10%, and electronics – 10%. China is the dominant consumer of selenium at 1,500–2,000 tonnes/year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Manganese_electrolysis">Manganese electrolysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Manganese electrolysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electro_winning" title="Electro winning">electro winning</a> of manganese, the addition of selenium dioxide decreases the power necessary to operate the <a href="/wiki/Electrolytic_cell" title="Electrolytic cell">electrolysis cells</a>. China is the largest consumer of selenium dioxide for this purpose. For every tonne of manganese, an average 2 kg selenium oxide is used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Glass_production">Glass production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Glass production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The largest commercial use of Se, accounting for about 50% of consumption, is for the production of glass. Se compounds confer a red color to glass. This color cancels out the green or yellow tints that arise from iron impurities typical for most glass. For this purpose, various selenite and selenate salts are added. For other applications, a red color may be desired, produced by mixtures of CdSe and CdS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alloys">Alloys</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Alloys">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Selenium is used with <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a> in <a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">brasses</a> to replace more toxic <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>. The regulation of lead in drinking water applications with the <a href="/wiki/Safe_Drinking_Water_Act" title="Safe Drinking Water Act">Safe Drinking Water Act</a> of 1974 made a reduction of lead in brass necessary. The new brass is marketed under the name EnviroBrass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Like lead and sulfur, selenium improves the machinability of steel at concentrations around 0.15%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Selenium produces the same machinability improvement in copper alloys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Lithium.E2.80.93selenium_batteries"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Lithium–selenium_batteries">Lithium–selenium batteries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Lithium–selenium batteries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lithium–selenium (Li–Se) battery is one of the most promising system for energy storage in the family of lithium batteries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Li–Se battery is an alternative to <a href="/wiki/Lithium%E2%80%93sulfur_battery" title="Lithium–sulfur battery">Lithium–sulfur battery</a> with an advantage of high electrical conductivity.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_cells">Solar cells</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Solar cells">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper_indium_gallium_selenide_solar_cell" title="Copper indium gallium selenide solar cell">Copper indium gallium selenide</a> is a material used in solar cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photoconductors">Photoconductors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Photoconductors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Amorphous selenium (α-Se) thin films have found application as photoconductors in <a href="/wiki/Flat_panel_detector" title="Flat panel detector">flat panel x-ray detectors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tan2006_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tan2006-64">[64]</a></sup>  These detectors utilize the amorphous selenium to capture and convert incident x-ray photons directly into electric charge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AGFA_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-AGFA-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rectifiers">Rectifiers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Rectifiers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Beginning from 1933 until the 1990s, Selenium was used for rectifiers, the such called <a href="/wiki/Selenium_rectifier" title="Selenium rectifier">selenium rectifiers</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_uses">Other uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Other uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Small amounts of organoselenium compounds have been used to modify the catalysts used for the <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_vulcanization" title="Sulfur vulcanization">vulcanization</a> for the production of rubber.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naumov_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Naumov-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>The demand for selenium by the electronics industry is declining.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-55">[55]</a></sup> Its <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">photovoltaic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Photoconductivity" title="Photoconductivity">photoconductive</a> properties are still useful in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photocopying" title="Photocopying">photocopying</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photocell" title="Photocell">photocells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Light_meter" title="Light meter">light meters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">solar cells</a>. Its use as a photoconductor in plain-paper copiers once was a leading application, but in the 1980s, the photoconductor application declined (although it was still a large end-use) as more and more copiers switched to organic photoconductors. Though once widely used, <a href="/wiki/Selenium_rectifier" title="Selenium rectifier">selenium rectifiers</a> have mostly been replaced (or are being replaced) by silicon-based devices. The most notable exception is in power DC <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surge_protection" title="Surge protection">surge protection</a>, where the superior energy capabilities of selenium suppressors make them more desirable than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal_oxide_varistor" title="Metal oxide varistor">metal oxide varistors</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Zinc_selenide" title="Zinc selenide">Zinc selenide</a> was the first material for blue <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LED" title="LED">LEDs</a>, but gallium nitride dominates that market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cadmium_selenide" title="Cadmium selenide">Cadmium selenide</a> was an important component in <a href="/wiki/Quantum_dot" title="Quantum dot">quantum dots</a>. Sheets of amorphous selenium convert <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> images to patterns of charge in <a href="/wiki/Xeroradiography" title="Xeroradiography">xeroradiography</a> and in solid-state, flat-panel X-ray cameras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Ionized selenium (Se+24) is one of the active mediums used in X-ray lasers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Selenium is a catalyst in some chemical reactions, but it is not widely used because of issues with toxicity. In <a href="/wiki/X-ray_crystallography" title="X-ray crystallography">X-ray crystallography</a>, incorporation of one or more selenium atoms in place of sulfur helps with multiple-wavelength anomalous dispersion and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Single_wavelength_anomalous_dispersion" title="Single wavelength anomalous dispersion">single wavelength anomalous dispersion</a> phasing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Selenium is used in the <a href="/wiki/Photographic_print_toning" title="Photographic print toning">toning of photographic prints</a>, and it is sold as a toner by numerous photographic manufacturers. Selenium intensifies and extends the tonal range of black-and-white photographic images and improves the permanence of prints.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p><sup>75</sup>Se is used as a gamma source in industrial radiography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_role">Biological role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Biological role">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Selenium_in_biology" title="Selenium in biology">Selenium in biology</a></div>

</td>
