E.g., liquid nitrogen"/><area alt="Special hazards (white): no code" coords="40,40,60,60,40,80,20,60" href="/wiki/NFPA_704#White" shape="poly" title="Special hazards (white): no code"/></map><img alt="NFPA 704 four-colored diamond" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" height="80" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/NFPA_704.svg/80px-NFPA_704.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/NFPA_704.svg/120px-NFPA_704.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/NFPA_704.svg/160px-NFPA_704.svg.png 2x" usemap="#ImageMap_1_1535398209" width="80"/></div></div><div style="width:12px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:12px; left:35px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Red" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Flammability code 0: Will not burn. E.g., water">0</span></a></div><div style="width:13px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:31px; left:15px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Blue" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Health code 2: Intense or continued but not chronic exposure could cause temporary incapacitation or possible residual injury. E.g., chloroform">2</span></a></div><div style="width:13px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:31px; left:54px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Yellow" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Reactivity code 0: Normally stable, even under fire exposure conditions, and is not reactive with water. E.g., liquid nitrogen">0</span></a></div></div></div></div>
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>Although it is toxic in large doses, selenium is an essential <a href="/wiki/Micronutrient" title="Micronutrient">micronutrient</a> for animals. In plants, it occurs as a bystander mineral<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2018)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, sometimes in toxic proportions in <a href="/wiki/Forage" title="Forage">forage</a> (some plants may accumulate selenium as a defense against being eaten by animals, but other plants, such as <a href="/wiki/Locoweed" title="Locoweed">locoweed</a>, require selenium, and their growth indicates the presence of selenium in soil).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruyle_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ruyle-5">[5]</a></sup> See more on plant nutrition below.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="plant nutrition below where? (November 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Selenium is a component of the unusual <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> <a href="/wiki/Selenocysteine" title="Selenocysteine">selenocysteine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Selenomethionine" title="Selenomethionine">selenomethionine</a>. In humans, selenium is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dietary_mineral" title="Dietary mineral">trace element</a> nutrient that functions as <a href="/wiki/Cofactor_(biochemistry)" title="Cofactor (biochemistry)">cofactor</a> for <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reduction</a> of <a href="/wiki/Antioxidant" title="Antioxidant">antioxidant</a> enzymes, such as <a href="/wiki/Glutathione_peroxidase" title="Glutathione peroxidase">glutathione peroxidases</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> and certain forms of <a href="/wiki/Thioredoxin_reductase" title="Thioredoxin reductase">thioredoxin reductase</a> found in animals and some plants (this enzyme occurs in all living organisms, but not all forms of it in plants require selenium).
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Glutathione_peroxidase" title="Glutathione peroxidase">glutathione peroxidase</a> family of enzymes (GSH-Px) catalyze certain reactions that remove reactive oxygen species such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">hydrogen peroxide</a> and organic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroperoxide" title="Hydroperoxide">hydroperoxides</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>2 GSH + H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>----GSH-Px → GSSG + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>The thyroid gland and every cell that uses thyroid hormone use selenium, which is a cofactor for the three of the four known types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thyroid_hormone" title="Thyroid hormone">thyroid hormone</a> <a href="/wiki/Deiodinase" title="Deiodinase">deiodinases</a>, which activate and then deactivate various thyroid hormones and their metabolites; the <a href="/wiki/Iodothyronine_deiodinase" title="Iodothyronine deiodinase">iodothyronine deiodinases</a> are the subfamily of deiodinase enzymes that use selenium as the otherwise rare amino acid selenocysteine. (Only the deiodinase, <a href="/wiki/Iodotyrosine_deiodinase" title="Iodotyrosine deiodinase">iodotyrosine deiodinase</a>, which works on the last breakdown products of thyroid hormone, does not use selenium.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Selenium may inhibit <a href="/wiki/Hashimoto%27s_thyroiditis" title="Hashimoto's thyroiditis">Hashimoto's disease</a>, in which the body's own thyroid cells are attacked as alien. A reduction of 21% on TPO antibodies is reported with the dietary intake of 0.2 mg of selenium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Increased dietary selenium reduces the effects of mercury toxicity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> although it is effective only at low to modest doses of mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Evidence suggests that the molecular mechanisms of mercury toxicity includes the irreversible inhibition of selenoenzymes that are required to prevent and reverse oxidative damage in brain and endocrine tissues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> An antioxidant, selenoneine, which is derived from selenium and has been found to be present in the blood of bluefin tuna, is the subject of scientific research regarding its possible roles in inflammatory and chronic diseases, methylmercury detoxification, and oxidative damages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_in_biology">Evolution in biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Evolution in biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolution_of_dietary_antioxidants" title="Evolution of dietary antioxidants">Evolution of dietary antioxidants</a></div>
<p>From about three billion years ago, prokaryotic selenoprotein families drive the evolution of selenocysteine, an amino acid. Selenium is incorporated into several prokaryotic selenoprotein families in bacteria, archaea, and eukaryotes as selenocysteine,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glady_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-glady-89">[89]</a></sup> where selenoprotein peroxiredoxins protect bacterial and eukaryotic cells against oxidative damage. Selenoprotein families of GSH-Px and the deiodinases of eukaryotic cells seem to have a bacterial <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetic</a> origin. The selenocysteine-containing form occurs in species as diverse as green algae, diatoms, sea urchin, fish, and chicken. Selenium enzymes are involved in the small reducing molecules <a href="/wiki/Glutathione" title="Glutathione">glutathione</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thioredoxin" title="Thioredoxin">thioredoxin</a>. One family of selenium-bearing molecules (the <a href="/wiki/Glutathione_peroxidase" title="Glutathione peroxidase">glutathione peroxidases</a>) destroys peroxide and repairs damaged peroxidized cell membranes, using glutathione. Another selenium-bearing enzyme in some plants and in animals (<a href="/wiki/Thioredoxin_reductase" title="Thioredoxin reductase">thioredoxin reductase</a>) generates reduced thioredoxin, a dithiol that serves as an electron source for peroxidases and also the important reducing enzyme <a href="/wiki/Ribonucleotide_reductase" title="Ribonucleotide reductase">ribonucleotide reductase</a> that makes DNA precursors from RNA precursors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>Trace elements involved in GSH-Px and superoxide dismutase enzymes activities, i.e. selenium, <a href="/wiki/Vanadium" title="Vanadium">vanadium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>, may have been lacking in some terrestrial mineral-deficient areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glady_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-glady-89">[89]</a></sup> Marine organisms retained and sometimes expanded their selenoproteomes, whereas the selenoproteomes of some terrestrial organisms were reduced or completely lost. These findings suggest that, with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>, aquatic life supports selenium use, whereas terrestrial habitats lead to reduced use of this trace element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Marine fishes and vertebrate thyroid glands have the highest concentration of selenium and iodine. From about 500 million years ago, freshwater and terrestrial plants slowly optimized the production of "new" endogenous antioxidants such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascorbic_acid" title="Ascorbic acid">ascorbic acid</a> (vitamin C), <a href="/wiki/Polyphenol" title="Polyphenol">polyphenols</a> (including flavonoids), <a href="/wiki/Tocopherol" title="Tocopherol">tocopherols</a>, etc. A few of these appeared more recently, in the last 50–200 million years, in fruits and flowers of angiosperm plants. In fact, the angiosperms (the dominant type of plant today) and most of their antioxidant pigments evolved during the late <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a> period.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The deiodinase <a href="/wiki/Isozyme" title="Isozyme">isoenzymes</a> constitute another family of eukaryotic selenoproteins with identified enzyme function. Deiodinases are able to extract electrons from iodides, and iodides from iodothyronines. They are, thus, involved in thyroid-hormone regulation, participating in the protection of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thyroid_epithelial_cell" title="Thyroid epithelial cell">thyrocytes</a> from damage by H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> produced for thyroid-hormone biosynthesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> About 200 million years ago, new selenoproteins were developed as mammalian GSH-Px enzymes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutritional_sources_of_selenium">Nutritional sources of selenium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Nutritional sources of selenium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Dietary selenium comes from nuts, cereals and mushrooms. <a href="/wiki/Brazil_nut" title="Brazil nut">Brazil nuts</a> are the richest dietary source (though this is soil-dependent, since the Brazil nut does not require high levels of the element for its own needs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>The U.S. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recommended_Dietary_Allowance" title="Recommended Dietary Allowance">Recommended Dietary Allowance</a> (RDA) for teenagers and adults is 55 µg/day.
Selenium as a dietary supplement is available in many forms, including multi-vitamins/mineral supplements, which typically contain 55 or 70 µg/serving.  Selenium-specific supplements typically contain either 100 or 200 µg/serving.
</p><p>In June 2015 the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> (FDA) published its final rule establishing the requirement of minimum and maximum levels of selenium in <a href="/wiki/Infant_formula" title="Infant formula">infant formula</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>The selenium content in the human body is believed to be in the 13–20 milligram range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indicator_plant_species">Indicator plant species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Indicator plant species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Certain species of plants are considered indicators of high selenium content of the soil because they require high levels of selenium to thrive. The main selenium indicator plants are <i><a href="/wiki/Astragalus" title="Astragalus">Astragalus</a></i> species (including some <a href="/wiki/Locoweed" title="Locoweed">locoweeds</a>), prince's plume (<i><a href="/wiki/Stanleya_(plant)" title="Stanleya (plant)">Stanleya</a></i> sp.), woody asters (<i><a href="/wiki/Xylorhiza" title="Xylorhiza">Xylorhiza</a></i> sp.), and false goldenweed (<i><a href="/wiki/Oonopsis" title="Oonopsis">Oonopsis</a></i> sp.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Detection_in_biological_fluids">Detection in biological fluids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Detection in biological fluids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Selenium may be measured in blood, plasma, serum, or urine to monitor excessive environmental or occupational exposure, to confirm a diagnosis of poisoning in hospitalized victims, or investigate a suspected case of fatal overdose. Some analytical techniques are capable of distinguishing organic from inorganic forms of the element. Both organic and inorganic forms of selenium are largely converted to monosaccharide conjugates (selenosugars) in the body prior elimination in the urine. Cancer patients receiving daily oral doses of selenothionine may achieve very high plasma and urine selenium concentrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicity">Toxicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Toxicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although selenium is an essential <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dietary_mineral" title="Dietary mineral">trace element</a>, it is toxic if taken in excess. Exceeding the <a href="/wiki/Dietary_Reference_Intake" title="Dietary Reference Intake">Tolerable Upper Intake Level</a> of 400 micrograms per day can lead to selenosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> This 400 <a href="/wiki/Microgram" title="Microgram">µg</a> Tolerable Upper Intake Level is based primarily on a 1986 study of five Chinese patients who exhibited overt signs of selenosis and a follow up study on the same five people in 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The 1992 study actually found the maximum safe dietary Se intake to be approximately 800 micrograms per day (15 micrograms per kilogram body weight), but suggested 400 micrograms per day to avoid creating an imbalance of nutrients in the diet and to accord with data from other countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> In China, people who ingested corn grown in extremely selenium-rich stony coal (carbonaceous <a href="/wiki/Shale" title="Shale">shale</a>) have suffered from selenium toxicity. This coal was shown to have selenium content as high as 9.1%, the highest concentration in coal ever recorded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Signs and symptoms of selenosis include a garlic odor on the breath, gastrointestinal disorders, hair loss, <a href="/wiki/Sloughing" title="Sloughing">sloughing</a> of nails, fatigue, irritability, and neurological damage. Extreme cases of selenosis can exhibit <a href="/wiki/Cirrhosis" title="Cirrhosis">cirrhosis</a> of the liver, <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_edema" title="Pulmonary edema">pulmonary edema</a>, or death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Elemental selenium and most metallic <a href="/wiki/Selenide" title="Selenide">selenides</a> have relatively low toxicities because of low <a href="/wiki/Bioavailability" title="Bioavailability">bioavailability</a>. By contrast, <a href="/wiki/Selenate" title="Selenate">selenates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Selenite_(ion)" title="Selenite (ion)">selenites</a> have an oxidant mode of action similar to that of arsenic trioxide and are very toxic. The chronic toxic dose of selenite for humans is about 2400 to 3000 micrograms of selenium per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_selenide" title="Hydrogen selenide">Hydrogen selenide</a> is an extremely toxic, corrosive gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Selenium also occurs in organic compounds, such as dimethyl selenide, <a href="/wiki/Selenomethionine" title="Selenomethionine">selenomethionine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Selenocysteine" title="Selenocysteine">selenocysteine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methylselenocysteine" title="Methylselenocysteine">methylselenocysteine</a>, all of which have high <a href="/wiki/Bioavailability" title="Bioavailability">bioavailability</a> and are toxic in large doses.
</p><p>On 19 April 2009, 21 <a href="/wiki/Polo" title="Polo">polo</a> ponies died shortly before a match in the United States Polo Open. Three days later, a pharmacy released a statement explaining that the horses had received an incorrect dose of one of the ingredients used in a vitamin/mineral supplement compound that had been incorrectly prepared by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compounding_pharmacy" title="Compounding pharmacy">compounding pharmacy</a>. Analysis of blood levels of <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_compound" title="Inorganic compound">inorganic compounds</a> in the supplement indicated the selenium concentrations were ten to fifteen times higher than normal in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_sample" title="Blood sample">blood samples</a>, and 15 to 20 times higher than normal in the liver samples. Selenium was later confirmed to be the toxic factor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selenium_Pollution" title="Selenium Pollution">Selenium poisoning</a> of water systems may result whenever new agricultural runoff courses through normally dry, undeveloped lands. This process leaches natural soluble selenium compounds (such as selenates) into the water, which may then be concentrated in new "wetlands" as the water evaporates. Selenium pollution of waterways also occurs when selenium is leached from coal flue ash, mining and metal smelting, crude oil processing, and landfill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The resultant high selenium levels in waterways were found to cause congenital disorders in oviparous species, including wetland birds<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> and fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Elevated dietary methylmercury levels can amplify the harm of selenium toxicity in oviparous species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Se_dose-response_curve_for_juvenile_salmon_mortality_-_percent_scale.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="627" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Se_dose-response_curve_for_juvenile_salmon_mortality_-_percent_scale.jpg/220px-Se_dose-response_curve_for_juvenile_salmon_mortality_-_percent_scale.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Se_dose-response_curve_for_juvenile_salmon_mortality_-_percent_scale.jpg/330px-Se_dose-response_curve_for_juvenile_salmon_mortality_-_percent_scale.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Se_dose-response_curve_for_juvenile_salmon_mortality_-_percent_scale.jpg/440px-Se_dose-response_curve_for_juvenile_salmon_mortality_-_percent_scale.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Se_dose-response_curve_for_juvenile_salmon_mortality_-_percent_scale.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Relationship between survival of juvenile salmon and concentration of selenium in their tissues after 90 days (Chinook salmon<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamilton_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hamilton-116">[116]</a></sup>) or 45 days (Atlantic salmon<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poston_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Poston-117">[117]</a></sup>) exposure to dietary selenium. The 10% lethality level (LC10=1.84 µg/g) was derived by applying the biphasic model of Brain and Cousens<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> to only the Chinook salmon data. The Chinook salmon data comprise two series of dietary treatments, combined here because the effects on survival are indistinguishable.</div></div></div>
<p>In fish and other wildlife, selenium is necessary for life, but toxic in high doses. For salmon, the optimal concentration of selenium is about 1 microgram selenium per gram of whole body weight. Much below that level, young salmon die from deficiency;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poston_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Poston-117">[117]</a></sup> much above, they die from toxic excess.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamilton_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hamilton-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">Occupational Safety and Health Administration</a> (OSHA) has set the legal limit (<a href="/wiki/Permissible_exposure_limit" title="Permissible exposure limit">Permissible exposure limit</a>) for selenium in the workplace at 0.2 mg/m<sup>3</sup> over an 8-hour workday. The <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health</a> (NIOSH) has set a <a href="/wiki/Recommended_exposure_limit" title="Recommended exposure limit">Recommended exposure limit</a> (REL) of 0.2 mg/m<sup>3</sup> over an 8-hour workday. At levels of 1 mg/m<sup>3</sup>, selenium is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IDLH" title="IDLH">immediately dangerous to life and health</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deficiency">Deficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Deficiency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Selenium_deficiency" title="Selenium deficiency">Selenium deficiency</a></div>
<p>Selenium deficiency can occur in patients with severely compromised <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intestine" title="Intestine">intestinal</a> function, those undergoing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Total_parenteral_nutrition" title="Total parenteral nutrition">total parenteral nutrition</a>, and<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> in those of advanced age (over 90). Also, people dependent on food grown from selenium-deficient soil are at risk. Although <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> soil has low levels of selenium, adverse health effects have not been detected in the residents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>Selenium deficiency, defined by low (&lt;60% of normal) selenoenzyme activity levels in brain and endocrine tissues, occurs only when a low selenium level is linked with an additional stress, such as high exposures to mercury<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> or increased oxidant stress from vitamin E deficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>Selenium interacts with other nutrients, such as <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">iodine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_E" title="Vitamin E">vitamin E</a>. The effect of selenium deficiency on health remains uncertain, particularly in relation to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kashin-Beck_disease" title="Kashin-Beck disease">Kashin-Beck disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Also, selenium interacts with other minerals, such as <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>. High doses of Se supplements in pregnant animals might disturb the Zn:Cu ratio and lead to Zn reduction; in such treatment cases, Zn levels should be monitored. Further studies are needed to confirm these interactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the regions (e.g. various regions within North America) where low selenium soil levels lead to low concentrations in the plants, some animal species may be deficient unless selenium is supplemented with diet or injection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ruminant" title="Ruminant">Ruminants</a> are particularly susceptible. In general, absorption of dietary selenium is lower in ruminants than other animals, and is lower from forages than from grain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NRCsr2007_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-NRCsr2007-127">[127]</a></sup> Ruminants grazing certain forages, e.g., some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_clover" title="White clover">white clover</a> varieties containing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyanogenic_glycoside" title="Cyanogenic glycoside">cyanogenic glycosides</a>, may have higher selenium requirements,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NRCsr2007_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-NRCsr2007-127">[127]</a></sup> presumably because cyanide is released from the <a href="/wiki/Aglycone" title="Aglycone">aglycone</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glucosidase" title="Glucosidase">glucosidase</a> activity in the rumen<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> and glutathione peroxidases is deactivated by the cyanide acting on the glutathione <a href="/wiki/Moiety_(chemistry)" title="Moiety (chemistry)">moiety</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Neonate ruminants at risk of <a href="/wiki/Nutritional_muscular_dystrophy" title="Nutritional muscular dystrophy">white muscle disease</a> may be administered both selenium and vitamin E by injection; some of the WMD <a href="/wiki/Myopathy" title="Myopathy">myopathies</a> respond only to selenium, some only to vitamin E, and some to either.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversial_health_effects">Controversial health effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selenium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Controversial health effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Selenium_in_biology" title="Selenium in biology">Selenium in biology</a></div>
<p>A number of correlative epidemiological studies have implicated selenium deficiency (measured by blood levels) in a number of serious or chronic diseases, such as cancer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jn.nutrition_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-jn.nutrition-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jn.nutrition_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-jn.nutrition-131">[131]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>. In addition, selenium supplementation has been found to be a chemopreventive for some types of cancer in some types of rodents.
One study of 118 exocrine pancreatic cancer (EPC) patients and 399 hospital controls in eastern Spain found high selenium concentrations to be inversely associated with the risk of EPC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Randomized_controlled_trial" title="Randomized controlled trial">randomized, blinded, controlled prospective trials</a> in humans, selenium supplementation has not succeeded in reducing the incidence of any disease, nor has a <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">meta-analysis</a> of such selenium supplementation studies detected a decrease in overall mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> However, selenium supplementation may be beneficial in cancer patients receiving <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiotherapy" title="Radiotherapy">radiotherapy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
